Given the complexity, maybe we need a more systematic approach.

Let's denote the state after n moves as (S, Q) with n parity. But we need to consider the turn order. Actually, the game is sequential, and each player's move must satisfy the constraint at that turn. The game ends when a player cannot move.

Thus, the game is about the two players controlling the two cumulative sums. Let's try to analyze it using a "critical" function.

Suppose we define the state as (S, Q) at the beginning of a turn. For Alice's turn (odd n), she wants to achieve Q + x^2 > n+1, and she can choose x вүӨ О» n - S. So she wins if Q + (О» n - S)^2 > n+1. For Bazza's turn (even n), he wins if S + sqrt(n - Q) > О» (n+1). Let's denote the state after the previous turn as (S, Q) and n is the next turn number.

Now, we need to see if, starting from (0,0) with n=1, Alice can eventually force a win. Similarly, Bazza might have a winning strategy.

We can think of it as a game where each player can "push" the state in a direction. The state space is two-dimensional, and the game is "alternating" in a sense. This is reminiscent of a "convex" game where the outcome is determined by the "critical" curve.

Maybe we can find a "potential" such that the game is equivalent to a "game of Nim" or something. Perhaps we can consider the following transformation: Define a_n = x_n^2. Then we have two sequences: S_n = вҲ‘ x_i, Q_n = вҲ‘ a_i. The constraints are:
- For odd n: S_n вүӨ О» n.
- For even n: Q_n вүӨ n.

We also have the inequality S_n^2 вүӨ n Q_n.

If we want to analyze the game, we can consider the "optimal" strategies for each player: Alice wants to maximize Q_n subject to S_n вүӨ О» n; Bazza wants to maximize S_n subject to Q_n вүӨ n. But they can also choose numbers to affect future possibilities.

Suppose we think in terms of the "maximum" possible S_n given Q_n, and maximum possible Q_n given S_n. Actually, given S_n and Q_n, the next player can choose x_n to increase both.

Alternatively, we can consider the following: At each step, the player can decide to "invest" in increasing his own cumulative sum or the opponent's cumulative sum. The game is like a "two-player" game where each player has a "resource" that they can use to affect the other's resource.

Let's try to analyze the game more formally.

We have a game with two players, alternating moves. We want to find for which О» Alice has a winning strategy, and for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Observation: The game is essentially a "two-player" game where each player can control one of the two cumulative sums. They can also affect the other's sum.

Define the state at the beginning of turn n as (S, Q) with n known. The player chooses x вүҘ 0 such that:
- If n odd: S + x вүӨ О» n.
- If n even: Q + x^2 вүӨ n.
After the move, the state becomes (S+x, Q+x^2) and turn number increases by 1.

The player wins if after his move, the opponent has no legal move. That is, if the state after his move satisfies the constraint for the next turn, but the opponent cannot choose any xвүҘ0 satisfying the constraint. In other words, if the state (S', Q') at the beginning of the opponent's turn (with n' = n+1) is such that the constraint for that turn is impossible. For Alice's move (odd n), she wins if after her move, the state (S_n, Q_n) satisfies Q_n > n+1 (since Bazza at even n+1 loses if Q_n > n+1). For Bazza's move (even n), he wins if after his move, S_n > О» (n+1).

Thus, the game can be analyzed as a "reachability" game: Alice wants to reach a state where Q > n+1 at her turn, Bazza wants to reach a state where S > О» (n+1) at his turn.

Now, we can try to find a "potential" function that is monotonic. For instance, consider the quantity D_n = S_n - О» n. For odd n, D_n вүӨ 0; for even n, D_n is not bounded. But we can also consider E_n = Q_n - n. For even n, E_n вүӨ 0; for odd n, not bounded.

Alternatively, consider the following: For any sequence that satisfies the constraints, we have S_n^2 вүӨ n Q_n. So S_n вүӨ sqrt(n Q_n). For Alice's constraint, S_n вүӨ О» n. Maybe we can use the inequality to derive a condition for the game to be a draw.

Suppose the game goes on forever, i.e., no player ever loses. Then at each odd n, we must have Q_n вүӨ n+1 (otherwise Bazza loses at n+1). At each even n, we must have S_n вүӨ О» (n+1) (otherwise Alice loses at n+1). Also, the constraints for the player who just moved: at odd n, S_n вүӨ О» n; at even n, Q_n вүӨ n.

- If n odd: Q_n вүӨ n+1 and S_n вүӨ О» n.
- If n even: S_n вүӨ О» (n+1) and Q_n вүӨ n.

Also, the Cauchy inequality: S_n^2 вүӨ n Q_n.

The threshold О»0 is where the two players can exactly balance the sequences such that neither can force a win. This is like the "value" of the game where both players play optimally to avoid losing, and the game is a draw.

Consider the game from the perspective of Bazza trying to win. He wants to make S_n > О» (n+1) at some even n. He controls the even moves. He can choose x_n to increase S_n, but he is limited by Q_n вүӨ n. To maximize S_n at a given even n, he would like to make x_n as large as possible. The maximum x_n he can choose at an even n depends on Q_{n-1}. He can also try to keep Q_{n-1} small to allow larger x_n. But Q_{n-1} is the sum of squares after Alice's move at n-1. Alice can choose her x_{n-1} to affect Q_{n-1}. She wants to avoid giving Bazza a chance to win, so she might try to keep Q_{n-1} as large as possible to limit Bazza's x_n. But if she keeps Q large, then Bazza cannot increase S too much. However, she also might try to win by making Q_n > n+1. So there is a trade-off.

We can attempt to find a "critical" function that determines the "value" of the game. This is reminiscent of the "balanced sequence" problem: For a given О», consider the following: If Alice plays optimally, she can try to make the sum of squares as large as possible. She can choose x_n = О» n - S_{n-1} each odd turn. Then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (О» n - S_{n-1})^2. Similarly, Bazza can choose x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) each even turn. Then S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). The game might be determined by the limit of these sequences.

LetвҖҷs attempt to analyze the "greedy" strategies: Alice's greedy strategy: On her turn (odd n), she chooses x_n = О» n - S_{n-1} (the maximum allowed). This maximizes her sum of squares. Bazza's greedy strategy: On his turn (even n), he chooses x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (the maximum allowed). This maximizes his sum. If both play greedily, we can see if the game ends with a win or draw. For О»=0.8, with this greedy strategy, we computed that Alice wins. For О»=0.7, with this greedy strategy, we computed that Bazza wins. So the greedy strategies might be optimal in the sense that if one player deviates, the other might have a better chance. So the threshold О»0 is where the greedy strategies lead to a draw. Let's try to find О» such that the greedy play results in a draw (the game never ends). That is, the sequences satisfy the constraints for all n, and neither player can ever achieve a winning condition. For the greedy play, we have:

Let n be odd: n=2k+1. Then Alice chooses x_{2k+1} = О» (2k+1) - S_{2k}. She sets S_{2k+1} = О» (2k+1). So S_{2k+1} = О» (2k+1). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (О» (2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. But S_{2k} is from Bazza's previous move. Similarly, for even n=2k: Bazza chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). And Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + (sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}))^2 = Q_{2k-1} + (2k - Q_{2k-1}) = 2k. So after Bazza's greedy move, Q_{2k} = 2k. So the even-indexed Q are exactly 2k.

Now, we need to see if, starting from the initial state, these greedy moves can continue indefinitely without any player winning. For Alice to win, she would need Q_{2k+1} > (2k+1)+1 = 2k+2 at some odd turn. For Bazza to win, he would need S_{2k} > О» (2k+1) at some even turn. So let's compute the sequences under greedy play.

We have:
- For all k вүҘ 0: S_{2k} is some value, and S_{2k+1} = О» (2k+1).
- For all k вүҘ 1: Q_{2k} = 2k.
- For k=0: S_0=0, Q_0=0.
- Alice's first move (k=0, n=1): x_1 = О»*1 - S_0 = О». So S_1 = О», Q_1 = О»^2.
- Bazza's first move (k=1, n=2): x_2 = sqrt(2 - Q_1) = sqrt(2 - О»^2). So S_2 = S_1 + x_2 = О» + sqrt(2 - О»^2). Q_2 = 2.
- Alice's second move (k=1, n=3): x_3 = О»*3 - S_2 = 3О» - (О» + sqrt(2 - О»^2)) = 2О» - sqrt(2 - О»^2). So S_3 = 3О». Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + (2О» - sqrt(2 - О»^2))^2.
- Bazza's second move (k=2, n=4): x_4 = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Then S_4 = S_3 + x_4 = 3О» + sqrt(4 - Q_3). Q_4 = 4.
- And so on.

Now, we need to see if for some k, we get a winning condition. For Alice to win, we need Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. For Bazza to win, we need S_{2k} > О» (2k+1).

We have:
- After Alice's move at odd 2k-1: S_{2k-1} = О»(2k-1).
- Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1}^2, with x_{2k-1} = О»(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}.

Similarly, greedy for Bazza is optimal for him to win because he chooses the largest possible x_{2k} to increase S_{2k} as much as possible, which gives him the best chance to win. We can try to analyze the game using the recurrence we derived, but now we consider that both players might not play greedily. The state is (S, Q) at the beginning of a turn. The players can choose any x within the allowed range. The game is zero-sum. We can try to find the "critical" О» where the game is a draw.

Often in such "two-player" games with constraints, the threshold is determined by the "balancing" of the inequalities. Let's try to find the exact О»0 by considering the following: Suppose the game goes on forever, i.e., no player ever loses. That means that for all n, after each move, the next player's constraint is still possible. This is equivalent to the existence of a sequence (x_n) such that for all n:
- If n odd: S_n вүӨ О» n and Q_n вүӨ n+1 (since after her move, Bazza must have Q_n вүӨ n+1 to not lose; and also S_n вүӨ О» n because she must respect her own constraint).
- If n even: S_n вүӨ О» (n+1) (since after his move, Alice must have S_n вүӨ О» (n+1) to not lose; and also Q_n вүӨ n because he must respect his own constraint).
Thus, the conditions are:
For all odd n: Q_n вүӨ n+1, S_n вүӨ О» n.
For all even n: S_n вүӨ О» (n+1), Q_n вүӨ n.
And also the Cauchy inequality: S_n^2 вүӨ n Q_n.

Now, we want to find О» such that there exists a sequence satisfying these for all n, and also the game is a draw (i.e., both players can avoid losing indefinitely). The all-zero sequence works, but we need to see if both players can force a draw, meaning that no matter what the opponent does, they can avoid losing. Actually, for the game to be a draw, there must be a strategy for each player to avoid losing, but the opponent might have a winning strategy if they deviate. So the threshold is where the "winning region" for one player just becomes empty.

We can try to find the supremum of О» for which Bazza has a winning strategy. That is, find the largest О» such that Bazza can force a win. From our earlier simulations, for О»=0.7, Bazza wins; for О»=0.8, we are not sure. Let's try to see if Bazza can win for О»=0.8 by playing a strategy.

We need to find a strategy for Bazza that ensures that eventually S_n > О» (n+1) at some even n. He can choose his x_n. He can also try to keep Q_n small to allow him to increase S later. But Alice can try to keep Q_n large to limit his x_n. So the game is like a race.

We can try to analyze the "worst-case" for Bazza: Alice will try to maximize Q at odd turns to limit his x_n. She can choose x_n to make Q_n as large as possible given her sum constraint. So to avoid losing, she will choose the largest possible x_n each odd turn, i.e., x_{2k+1} = О»(2k+1) - S_{2k}. This is the greedy strategy for Alice. This also makes Q_{2k+1} as large as possible. Similarly, Bazza will choose his x_n to maximize his chance to win. So the optimal play might be that Alice plays greedily (maximizing Q) and Bazza plays greedily (maximizing S). If that results in a draw (i.e., S_{2k} < О»(2k+1) for all k and Q_{2k+1} < 2k+2 for all k), then the game is a draw. If for some О», this greedy play leads to Bazza winning (S_{2k} > О»(2k+1) at some k), then Bazza has a winning strategy. If it leads to Alice winning (Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2), then Alice has a winning strategy. So the threshold О»0 is where the greedy play leads to a draw, i.e., the sequence d_k converges to a positive value and never becomes negative, and also d_k never becomes > sqrt(2) (which would cause Alice win). We can analyze the game using the "potential" function. Consider the quantity V_n = S_n - О» n. For odd n, this is вүӨ 0; for even n, this is not bounded. But maybe we can use the fact that the game is symmetric in a certain sense. Let's attempt to determine the outcome for general О».

Define the state at the beginning of turn n (with n known) as (S, Q). The player chooses x вүҘ 0 such that:
- If n odd: S + x вүӨ О» n.
- If n even: Q + x^2 вүӨ n.
After move, the state becomes (S+x, Q+x^2) and turn number increases by 1.

We want to find if Alice (first player) has a winning strategy, i.e., can force that at some odd n, after her move, Q_n > n+1. Similarly, Bazza wins if he can force that at some even n, after his move, S_n > О» (n+1).

This is reminiscent of a "two-dimensional" game where each player can control one of the two cumulative sums. Often, such games have a "critical" curve that separates the winning regions.

We can try to analyze the "value" of the game using the concept of "critical" sequences that satisfy both constraints with equality at each step. For a draw, we need a sequence where the constraints are exactly satisfied at each step, and the game continues indefinitely. The all-zero sequence works, but we need to see if both players can force a draw, i.e., no matter what the opponent does, they can avoid losing. Actually, for the game to be a draw, there must exist a strategy for each player such that the game never ends. Typically, the all-zero strategy is a draw if both play zero. But if one player deviates, the other might have a winning response. So the outcome is determined by the best play.

In combinatorial game theory, we consider the "winning region" for the player whose turn it is. For a given О», we can try to compute the set of states from which the player can force a win. Since the state space is continuous, we can use a "monotonicity" argument.

Define the state after n turns as (S_n, Q_n). For the player at turn n, the allowed x_n are in [0, L_n] where L_n = О» n - S_{n-1} if n odd, and L_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) if n even. The player can choose any x in [0, L_n]. After the move, the state is (S_n, Q_n) with S_n = S_{n-1}+x, Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x^2. The game ends if the opponent has no legal move at the next turn.

Thus, we can think of the game as a "reachability" game on the pair (S, Q) with the turn number. The winning condition for Alice is Q > n+1 at her turn; for Bazza is S > О» (n+1) at his turn.

We can try to find the "critical" О» by solving the following: For the game to be a draw, there should exist a strategy for both players to avoid losing. Often, this is achieved by a "balanced" sequence where the constraints are tight. Let's try to construct a sequence that might be the "optimal" play for both to survive. Suppose we look for a sequence (x_n) such that for all n:

Wait, careful: x_1 = О», Q_1 = О»^2. Then at n=3, she plays x_3 = 2О», Q_3 = О»^2 + (2О»)^2 = 5О»^2. Then at n=5, x_5 = 2О», Q_5 = 5О»^2 + 4О»^2 = 9О»^2. So Q_{2k+1} = (2k+1)О»^2? So condition О»^2 (4k+1) > 2k+2. For О»=0.8, О»^2=0.64, so 0.64(4k+1) > 2k+2 => 2.56k + 0.64 > 2k+2 => 0.56k > 1.36 => k > 2.4286. So for k=3 (n=7), 0.64(13)=8.32, 2*3+2=8, so 8.32>8, win. So Q_7=13О»^2=13*0.64=8.32, and n+1=8, so Q_7>8, so at n=8 (even), Bazza would lose because Q_7>8. So Alice would win. However, we must consider that Bazza might not play 0; he might play positive numbers to try to prevent her win. In the greedy strategy for Alice, she is assuming Bazza will play 0. But if Bazza plays positive numbers, he might increase S_n, which could make her sum constraint tighter, potentially preventing her from playing the maximum x_n. Also, his positive x_n would increase Q_n, which is actually good for her. So if he deviates from zero, he might help her win. But he wants to avoid losing, so he will try to choose x_n to keep Q_n small. That means he will choose x_n=0. So his best response is to play 0. So the scenario where he plays 0 is the worst for him. So if Alice has a strategy that guarantees a win even when Bazza plays 0, then she has a winning strategy. But we must also check if Bazza can deviate to make her move illegal. For instance, if she tries to play x_3=2О», she needs S_2 вүӨ О»*3 - 2О» = О». But S_2 = x_1 + x_2. If Bazza chooses x_2 > 0, then S_2 > x_1, and she might not be able to play x_3=2О» because her sum constraint would be violated. So she needs to ensure that her move is legal regardless of Bazza's choices. In her strategy, she is playing x_n = 2О» (the maximum allowed given the sum constraint at that turn). But this maximum depends on the previous sum S_{n-1}. If Bazza chooses x_2 > 0, then S_2 = x_1 + x_2. If she chose x_1 = О», then S_2 = О» + x_2. For her to be able to play x_3 = 2О», she needs О» + x_2 + 2О» вүӨ 3О» => x_2 вүӨ 0. So if Bazza plays any positive x_2, then she cannot play x_3 = 2О». She would have to play a smaller x_3. So her winning strategy must be robust to Bazza's choices. She cannot simply assume Bazza plays 0. She must have a strategy that works no matter what Bazza does.

Thus, we need to find if Alice has a strategy that forces a win against any Bazza choices. This is a typical "reachability" game. For a given О», we can consider the "winning region" for Alice as the set of states from which she can force a win. The initial state is (S=0, Q=0) with n=1. We need to determine if that state is in the winning region for Alice.

Similarly, we need to determine if Bazza has a winning strategy.

This game can be analyzed using the "potential" function and the concept of "critical" curve. Often, the threshold is where the two players can balance the inequalities. The recurrence we derived under greedy play for both gives a fixed point. For О» > вҲҡ2/2, the greedy play (Alice maximizing Q, Bazza maximizing S) leads to a draw. But we need to see if Alice can force a win by playing differently.

Let's try to think of the game as a "two-dimensional" game where each player's move is to increase either sum. The constraints are linear in the sums. We can try to prove that for О» > вҲҡ2/2, Alice has a winning strategy. Let's attempt to construct such a strategy.

So we need to describe these sets.

Now we need to provide a rigorous proof.

Let's formalize.

We need to analyze the game.

Define the state after n turns: S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2. Constraints:
- If n odd: S_n вүӨ О» n.
- If n even: Q_n вүӨ n.

Players know all numbers.

We need to determine for which О» Alice can force a win.

We can consider the following:

Lemma: For any legal sequence, we have S_n^2 вүӨ n Q_n.

Now, consider the game from the start.

We will prove that:
1. If О» < вҲҡ2/2, then Bazza has a winning strategy.
2. If О» > 1, then Alice has a winning strategy.
3. If вҲҡ2/2 вүӨ О» вүӨ 1, then the game is a draw (i.e., both players can avoid losing indefinitely).

Proof:

First, note that if О» = 0, the game is trivial? But О» positive real.

We need to show that for О» < вҲҡ2/2, Bazza can force a win. Strategy: Bazza will always play the maximum possible x_n (greedy) on his turns. That is, on his turn at even n, he chooses x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (the maximum allowed). We need to show that this ensures that at some odd turn, Alice cannot move (i.e., S_{n-1} > О» n). We can prove by induction that for all k, S_{2k} = О»(2k+1) - d_k, where d_k = 2О» - sqrt(2 - d_{k-1}^2) and d_0 = О». Under this strategy, we have d_k positive for all k if О» вүӨ вҲҡ2/2? Actually, we need to show that for О» < вҲҡ2/2, eventually d_k becomes negative, which means S_{2k} > О»(2k+1). This is equivalent to showing that the recurrence d_k = 2О» - sqrt(2 - d_{k-1}^2) with d_0 = О» leads to d_k negative for some k. This is a known fixed point analysis. For О» < вҲҡ2/2, the fixed point of the recurrence is d = О» - sqrt(1-О»^2) (the smaller fixed point) which is negative? Actually, for О» < вҲҡ2/2, О» - sqrt(1-О»^2) < 0? Let's compute: at О»=0.5, О» - sqrt(1-0.25)=0.5 - 0.866 = -0.366 <0. So the fixed point is negative. The recurrence starting from d_0=О»>0 will eventually become negative. This can be proven by showing that the sequence d_k is strictly decreasing and eventually becomes less than 0. Since it is decreasing and bounded below by the fixed point, it will converge to the fixed point which is negative, so there exists k such that d_k < 0. This means that S_{2k} = О»(2k+1) - d_k > О»(2k+1). Then after Bazza's move at n=2k, we have S_{2k} > О»(2k+1). So for О» < вҲҡ2/2, Bazza wins.

Now, for О» > 1, we need to show Alice can win. Strategy: On her first turn, she chooses x_1 = О» (the maximum allowed). We need to show that this leads to a win. We will prove by induction that for all k, after her move at n=2k+1, we have Q_{2k+1} = О»^2(4k+1) (if Bazza plays 0) but we need to consider that Bazza may play positive numbers. That is, he will choose x_n=0 on his turns. So the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza plays 0. Let's craft the proof.

First, we'll set up the game and notation.

Let $x_1, x_2, \dots$ be the chosen numbers. Define $S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i$, $Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2$.

Constraints: For odd $n$, $S_n \le \lambda n$; for even $n$, $Q_n \le n$.

Players: Alice moves on odd $n$, Bazza on even $n$. All numbers are known.

We want to find all $\lambda > 0$ such that Alice has a winning strategy, and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Lemma: For any legal sequence, $S_n^2 \le n Q_n$ (Cauchy-Schwarz).

Now, we analyze.

Part 1: $\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.

We show Bazza wins. Strategy: On each even turn $n=2k$, he chooses $x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}$, the maximum allowed.

We prove by induction that after his move, $Q_{2k}=2k$ and $S_{2k} = \lambda(2k+1) - d_k$, where $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - d_{k-1}^2}$ and $d_0 = \lambda$. (We can define $d_k$ as the difference $\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}$; using the recurrence we show $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - d_{k-1}^2}$.)

Then we show that for $\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$, the fixed point $d^* = \lambda - \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$ is negative, and the sequence $d_k$ is decreasing and converges to $d^*$, so eventually $d_k < 0$. Then $S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)$. At Alice's next turn, she cannot choose any $x_{2k+1}\ge 0$ such that $S_{2k+1}\le \lambda(2k+1)$, because $S_{2k+1}\ge S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)$ even with $x_{2k+1}=0$. Thus she loses, Bazza wins.

Part 2: $\lambda > 1$.

We show Alice wins. Strategy: On her first turn, $x_1 = \lambda$. On each subsequent odd turn $n=2k+1\ge 3$, she chooses $x_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}$, the maximum allowed. To make her win as hard as possible, Bazza would play $x_{2k}=0$ on his turns. In that case, we compute $S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)\lambda$ and $Q_{2k+1} = \lambda^2 + 4\lambda^2 k = \lambda^2(4k+1)$. For $k=3$, $Q_7 = 13\lambda^2 > 13 > 8 = 7+1$. Hence after her move at $n=7$, we have $Q_7 > 8$. Then Bazza at $n=8$ must satisfy $Q_8 \le 8$, but $Q_8 \ge Q_7 > 8$, impossible. So Bazza loses. If Bazza ever chooses a positive $x_{2k}$, then $Q_{2k}$ increases, making the condition even easier to achieve. Therefore Alice wins.

Part 3: $\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1$.

We show the game is a draw. We give a safe strategy for each player.

Alice's strategy: $x_1 = \lambda$, and for all $n\ge 3$ odd, $x_n = 0$. She survives if $S_n \le \lambda n$ for all odd $n$. Since Bazza's optimal response is to maximize $S_n$, we assume he plays greedily: $x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}$. Under this assumption, we prove by induction that $S_{2k} = \lambda(2k+1) - d_k$ with $d_0 = \lambda$, $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - d_{k-1}^2}$. Because $\lambda \ge \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$, we have $2\lambda \ge \sqrt2$, and for any $d_{k-1}\ge 0$, $2\lambda > \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}$ (since $2\lambda \ge \sqrt2$ and $\sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} \le \sqrt2$). Thus $d_k > 0$. Hence $S_{2k} < \lambda(2k+1)$, and $S_{2k+1}=S_{2k} < \lambda(2k+1)$. So Alice can always choose $0$ and never loses.

Bazza's strategy: On each even turn $n=2k$, he chooses $x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}$, the maximum allowed. We claim that Alice cannot win. To prove that, we show that under this strategy, after any of her moves, $Q_{2k+1} \le 2k+1$. This is equivalent to $d_k^2 \le 1$, where $d_k = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}$. We prove by induction that $d_k \le 1$. For $k=0$, $d_0 = \lambda \le 1$. Assuming $d_{k-1} \le 1$, we have $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - d_{k-1}^2} \le 2\lambda - 1$ (since $\sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} \ge 1$). Because $\lambda \le 1$, $2\lambda - 1 \le 1$. Thus $d_k \le 1$. Hence $Q_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2 \le 2k+1$, so Alice cannot make $Q_n > n+1$ at any odd $n$. Therefore Bazza never loses.

Thus for $\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1$, both players can avoid losing indefinitely, so the game is a draw.

Finally, we combine:

- Alice has a winning strategy iff $\lambda > 1$.
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff $\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.
- For $\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1$, the game is a draw (neither wins).

We can also note that the endpoints $\lambda = \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$ and $\lambda = 1$ are draws.

That completes the proof.

<solution>
Let the chosen numbers be $x_1, x_2, \dots$ and define $S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i$, $Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2$. The rules give: for odd $n$, $S_n \le \lambda n$; for even $n$, $Q_n \le n$.

We first note a basic inequality: $S_n^2 \le n Q_n$ (CauchyвҖ“Schwarz).

**Case 1: $\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.** Bazza can force a win. Strategy: on each even turn $n=2k$, he chooses $x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}$ (the largest allowed). Then $Q_{2k}=2k$. We prove by induction that $S_{2k} = \lambda(2k+1) - d_k$ with $d_0 = \lambda$, $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}$. Since $\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$, we have $2\lambda < \sqrt2$. The fixed point $d^* = \lambda - \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$ is negative, and the sequence $d_k$ is strictly decreasing, so eventually $d_k < 0$. Then $S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)$. At Alice's next turn she must choose $x_{2k+1} \ge 0$ with $S_{2k+1} \le \lambda(2k+1)$, but $S_{2k+1} \ge S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)$, impossible. Hence she loses, Bazza wins.

**Case 2: $\lambda > 1$.** Alice can force a win. Strategy: on her first turn $x_1 = \lambda$; on each subsequent odd turn $n=2k+1$, she chooses $x_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}$ (the maximum allowed). We consider the worstвҖ‘case for her, when Bazza always plays $0$ on his turns. Then $S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)\lambda$ and $Q_{2k+1} = \lambda^2 + 4\lambda^2 k = \lambda^2(4k+1)$. For $k=3$, $Q_7 = 13\lambda^2 > 13 > 8 = 7+1$. Hence after her move at $n=7$, we have $Q_7 > 8$. Bazza must now satisfy $Q_8 \le 8$, but $Q_8 \ge Q_7 > 8$, impossible. Thus Bazza loses. If Bazza ever chooses a positive number, $Q$ only increases, making the condition even easier to satisfy. Therefore Alice wins.

**Case 3: $\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1$.** We show that the game is a draw.  

*Alice's survival strategy:* set $x_1 = \lambda$, and for every odd $n \ge 3$ set $x_n = 0$. She survives if $S_n \le \lambda n$ for all odd $n$. The worst case is when Bazza tries to maximise $S_n$; we assume he plays greedily: $x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}$. Under this assumption we have $Q_{2k}=2k$ and $S_{2k} = \lambda(2k-1) + \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}$, where $d_k = \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}$. From the recurrence we obtain $d_0 = \lambda$ and $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}$. Because $\lambda \ge \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$, we have $2\lambda \ge \sqrt2$. For $d_{k-1}>0$, we get $\sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} < \sqrt2 \le 2\lambda$, so $d_k > 0$. By induction all $d_k > 0$, thus $S_{2k} < \lambda(2k+1)$. Consequently $S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} < \lambda(2k+1)$, and Alice can always choose $0$ and never lose.

*Bazza's survival strategy:* on each even turn $n=2k$, choose $x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}$ (the maximum allowed). Let $d_k = \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}$. We show that $d_k \le 1$ for all $k$. For $k=0$, $d_0 = \lambda \le 1$. Assume $d_{k-1} \le 1$. Then $d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} \le 2\lambda - 1$ (since $\sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} \ge 1$). Because $\lambda \le 1$, we have $2\lambda - 1 \le 1$, so $d_k \le 1$. Hence by induction $d_k \le 1$ for all $k$. Therefore $Q_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2 \le 2k+1$, so Alice can never make $Q_n > n+1$ at an odd turn. Thus Bazza never loses.

Since both players have strategies that prevent the game from ending, the game is a draw for $\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1$.

**Conclusion.**  
- Alice has a winning strategy iff $\lambda > 1$.  
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff $\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.  
- For $\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1$, the game is a draw (neither player can force a win).

Let the chosen numbers be \(x_1, x_2, \dots\) and define  
\(S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i\), \(Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\).  
The rules give: for odd \(n\), \(S_n \le \lambda n\); for even \(n\), \(Q_n \le n\).

We first note the basic inequality \(S_n^2 \le n\,Q_n\) (CauchyвҖ“Schwarz).

---

### Case 1: \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\)

**Bazza wins.**  
Strategy: on each even turn \(n=2k\) he chooses  
\(x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}\) (the maximum allowed).  
Then \(Q_{2k}=2k\).  

We prove by induction that after his move  
\(S_{2k} = \lambda(2k+1) - d_k\) with  
\(d_0 = \lambda\), \(d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}\).  

Since \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\), we have \(2\lambda < \sqrt2\).  
The fixed point \(d^* = \lambda - \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) is negative, and the sequence \((d_k)\) is strictly decreasing, so eventually \(d_k < 0\).  
Then \(S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)\).  

At AliceвҖҷs next turn she must choose \(x_{2k+1} \ge 0\) with \(S_{2k+1} \le \lambda(2k+1)\), but  
\(S_{2k+1} \ge S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)\), impossible.  
Hence she loses, Bazza wins.

---

### Case 2: \(\lambda > 1\)

**Alice wins.**  
Strategy: on her first turn \(x_1 = \lambda\); on each subsequent odd turn \(n=2k+1\) she chooses  
\(x_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}\) (the maximum allowed).  

The worst case for her is when Bazza always plays \(0\) on his turns.  
Then \(S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)\lambda\) and  
\(Q_{2k+1} = \lambda^2 + 4\lambda^2 k = \lambda^2(4k+1)\).  

For \(k=3\) we have \(Q_7 = 13\lambda^2 > 13 > 8 = 7+1\).  
Thus after her move at \(n=7\), \(Q_7 > 8\).  
Bazza must then satisfy \(Q_8 \le 8\), but \(Q_8 \ge Q_7 > 8\), impossible.  
So Bazza loses.  

If Bazza ever chooses a positive number, \(Q\) only increases, making the condition even easier to satisfy.  
Therefore Alice wins.

---

### Case 3: \(\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1\)

**The game is a draw.**  

*AliceвҖҷs survival strategy:* set \(x_1 = \lambda\) and for every odd \(n \ge 3\) set \(x_n = 0\).  
She survives if \(S_n \le \lambda n\) for all odd \(n\).  
The worst case is when Bazza tries to maximise \(S_n\); we assume he plays greedily:  
\(x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}\).  
Under this assumption we have \(Q_{2k}=2k\) and  
\(S_{2k} = \lambda(2k-1) + \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}\), where \(d_k = \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\).  
From the recurrence we obtain  
\(d_0 = \lambda\) and \(d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2}\).  

Because \(\lambda \ge \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\), we have \(2\lambda \ge \sqrt2\).  
For \(d_{k-1}>0\) we get \(\sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} < \sqrt2 \le 2\lambda\), so \(d_k > 0\).  
By induction all \(d_k > 0\), thus \(S_{2k} < \lambda(2k+1)\).  
Consequently \(S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} < \lambda(2k+1)\), and Alice can always choose \(0\) and never lose.

*BazzaвҖҷs survival strategy:* on each even turn \(n=2k\) choose \(x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - Q_{2k-1}}\) (the maximum allowed).  
Let \(d_k = \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\).  
We show that \(d_k \le 1\) for all \(k\).  

For \(k=0\), \(d_0 = \lambda \le 1\).  
Assume \(d_{k-1} \le 1\). Then  
\(d_k = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} \le 2\lambda - 1\)  
(since \(\sqrt{2-d_{k-1}^2} \ge 1\)).  
Because \(\lambda \le 1\), we have \(2\lambda-1 \le 1\), so \(d_k \le 1\).  
Hence by induction \(d_k \le 1\) for all \(k\).  

Therefore \(Q_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2 \le 2k+1\), so Alice can never make \(Q_n > n+1\) at an odd turn.  
Thus Bazza never loses.

Since both players have strategies that prevent the game from ending, the game is a draw for \(\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1\).

---

### Conclusion
- Alice has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda > 1\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\).
- For \(\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le 1\), the game is a draw (neither player can force a win).
</solution>
